  <span id="freeText12141652215277825188" style="display:none">Normally, when a book screams at me, <i>I'm going to be a movie, someday!</i> I let it go. In the case of <i> Defending Jacob</i>, I sullenly read on, primarily because it was this month's selection for my book club at the Adult Center <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <a href="http://media.photobucket.com/image/old%20people/dskiser/badpics.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380388753i/749528.jpg" alt="Photobucket Pictures, Images and Photos" class="gr-hostedUserImg">

 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.I would no sooner have shown up on Tuesday AM to face this band of retired teachers and librarians without having read the book, than I would have smoked pot before taking the SAT's. <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380388753i/749529._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> So on I read, becoming increasingly annoyed as implausible plot twists piled one on top of the other. The first-person narrator is a seasoned district attorney who finds himself on the other side when his son is accused of murdering a classmate. There are descriptions of affluent suburban life and the hell of middle school which lend it a breezy relevance, but according to my lawyer friends the legal proceedings strain credulity. I see things through the prism of literature, not the law, so I enjoyed all the philosophical musings about a murder gene which puts this squarely into the realm of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nathaniel_Hawthorne" rel="nofollow noopener">Nathaniel Hawthorne </a>and his fixation on the sins of our fathers.<img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380388753i/749530._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> There are also some insights into the risks and rigors of parenting that hit me in the heart, but overall I found this to be predictable and a bit too long. It was like a two-hour season finale of a TV crime show, with a lot of extraneous padding to allow for more commercials.</span>

<div id="review_1457740374" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1457740374" />
    <a title="Snotchocheez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1899836-snotchocheez"><img alt="Snotchocheez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510561146p2/1899836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1457740374?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Snotchocheez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Snotchocheez" href="/user/show/1899836-snotchocheez">Snotchocheez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1457740374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2534235316336910959">I should probably "plead the fifth" on this one, as so many of my friends enjoyed this legal....thriller? mystery? angsty hand-wringer? <i> Defending Jacob</i> didn't really work on any level for me, sad to say. William Landay's many years employed as a DA are evident here, as the courtroom scenes are competently written, but implausibilities abound (as does really awful dialogue, particularly amongst adults, curiously enough). The end result is (as one keen observer noted in a one-word dismissal:) Shl</span>
  <span id="freeText2534235316336910959" style="display:none">I should probably "plead the fifth" on this one, as so many of my friends enjoyed this legal....thriller? mystery? angsty hand-wringer? <i> Defending Jacob</i> didn't really work on any level for me, sad to say. William Landay's many years employed as a DA are evident here, as the courtroom scenes are competently written, but implausibilities abound (as does really awful dialogue, particularly amongst adults, curiously enough). The end result is (as one keen observer noted in a one-word dismissal:) Shlocky. <br /><br />With about as much subtlety as Nancy Grace (or most anything airing on HLN, really), Landay feeds on the fears of a suburban parent's worst nightmare: the murder of a child. Jacob Barber, a 14 year-old Newton, MA geeky, sullen 8th grader is the accused; his father, Andy, is the the county's ADA who can't possibly imagine his son could've killed a classmate (even though signals abound that he clearly was capable of it.) The awkward trial (with a man ordinarily tasked with putting people behind bars now having to fight to keep his son from being thrown behind bars) ensues. A few (transparent, if not vivid neon) red herrings are thrown into the procedings, ostensibly to keep the reader off-balance, wondering if Jacob really did it. As the trial progressed, though, I (thanks to the introduction of some sketchy pseudo-science via the topic of heredity of "the murder gene", and some laughably awful, stilted give-and-take between Jacob's parents) found myself not really caring a whit about Jacob's "plight". A few curve balls thrown toward the end <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ like the grandfather, while in prison, arranging a fixer to "fix" Jacob's quickly-tanking court case; or the Joren van der Sloot-y Jamaican family holiday; or Jacob's mom's showing her love by crashing into a bridge embankment <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> seemed painfully contrived, rather than shocking like Landay obviously intended.<br /><br />I apologize to my friends (and there are many) who gave <i> Defending Jacob</i> 4 or 5 stars, but I just could not feel the love for this. Maybe the movie adaptation they're planning will prove to be one of those rare cases of the-movie-is-better-than-the-book. I, for one, am not exactly champing at the bit to find out, though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2534235316336910959" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2125773089" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2125773089" />
    <a title="Joy D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d"><img alt="Joy D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450639623p2/50297885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2125773089?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy D" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d">Joy D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11700464-defending-jacob">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=mental-health">mental-health</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=family-dynamics">family-dynamics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=library-audio">library-audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=law">law</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=north-america">north-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2125773089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13387905787933038356">In 2007 in Newton, Massachusetts, a fourteen-year-old boy is murdered on his way to school. Jacob Barber, a classmate and son of Assistant District Attorney Andrew Barber, is accused of the crime. Another suspect gets put aside, perhaps mistakenly, when the investigation focuses on Jacob. The plot revolves around Jacob’s trial, the family’s experiences, and a grand jury hearing whose purpose remains unclear until near the end of the book. <br/><br/>There is a lot going on in this book. The story weaves to</span>
  <span id="freeText13387905787933038356" style="display:none">In 2007 in Newton, Massachusetts, a fourteen-year-old boy is murdered on his way to school. Jacob Barber, a classmate and son of Assistant District Attorney Andrew Barber, is accused of the crime. Another suspect gets put aside, perhaps mistakenly, when the investigation focuses on Jacob. The plot revolves around Jacob’s trial, the family’s experiences, and a grand jury hearing whose purpose remains unclear until near the end of the book. <br /><br />There is a lot going on in this book. The story weaves together a number of threads, including Andrew’s estranged relationship with his father, multiple conversations with a psychiatrist, whether Andrew Barber engaged in any conflicts of interest, Andrew’s unauthorized pursuit of an alternate suspect, and internal politics in the DA’s office. It explores themes of family loyalty, parental responsibility, and nature versus nurture. <br /><br />Dramatic tension is maintained by the ongoing doubts about Jacob’s guilt. It is compounded by the torments experienced by his family after he is accused of murder. Andrew Barber serves as narrator, interspersed with excerpts from the trial transcript. Andrew maintains a steadfast belief in his son’s innocence. The trial conveys evidence in the manner of a true court case, alternating between the prosecution and the defense. This works well in keeping the reader guessing. <br /><br />I was drawn into the story, closely paying attention to every nuance. Its primary strength is not giving anything away too early. The writing is direct. I enjoyed the complexities. I was not overly fond of the ending, but I can understand the reasoning, as it allows the reader to judge the outcome. I listened to the audio book read by Grover Gardner. His voice fits the material. He reads quickly and clearly. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13387905787933038356" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_506869436" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Mary Beth " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14233569-mary-beth"><img alt="Mary Beth " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479858526p2/14233569.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary Beth " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary Beth " href="/user/show/14233569-mary-beth">Mary Beth </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4773438691035542975">If you have been like me and have this on your Tbr like forever, I think you might want to read it. I don't know what took so long for me to read this. It was a big surprise. I loved this and its a really great book.<br/><br/>Its been awhile since I've read a good courtroom thriller and I will be reading more books by this author<br/>It also has given me the urge to want to read more legal thrillers. <br/><br/>This book played very well on my emotions.   <span id="freeText4773438691035542975" style="display:none">If you have been like me and have this on your Tbr like forever, I think you might want to read it. I don't know what took so long for me to read this. It was a big surprise. I loved this and its a really great book.<br /><br />Its been awhile since I've read a good courtroom thriller and I will be reading more books by this author<br />It also has given me the urge to want to read more legal thrillers. <br /><br />This book played very well on my emotions. It kept me in suspense, turning the pages till late at night.<br /><br />Andy Barbour is the Assistant District Attorney.<br />He is called to a crime scene where Ben Rifkin, a 14 year old boy has been stabbed in a park. The police think they have a suspect, a pedophile who lives nearby and is known to go to the park often.<br />Then Andy's son Jacob, one of Bens classmates becomes a suspect. Ben was known to be a mean kid and a bully. He had been harassing Jacob. They think that this gives Jacob a motive to murder Ben. A lot of Jacob's classmates post messages on facebook suggesting he is guilty of the crime. They also commented that he showed his knife to his friends. When Andy finds the knife he quickly gets rid of it.<br /><br />Laurie Jacob's Mom is half convinced that her son is guilty due to the fact that Andy's father and grandfather have been murderers. She fears that Jacob may have inherited, a gene associated with aggressive behaviors in makes.<br /><br />If you like legal thrillers with twists and turns, suspense never ending this book is for you. I gave it 4 stars but feel that it was a little wordy and could of been a little shorter. Other than that I would of given it 5 stars.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4773438691035542975" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('VRLdZ5jspKwLlgq+9otyhpIkTqA84cSu1gFhF2ifx4Npws20xBKhz/SGCaaoBnl71MYdfyD57C8+55eff7ssBg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

Previous novels include <i>Mission Flats</i>, winner of the Dagger Award for best debut crime novel, and <i>The Strangler</i>, which was an <i>L.A. Times</i> favorite crime novel and nominated for the <i>Strand Magazine</i> Critics Award as best crime novel of the year.</span>
